Development Corporation (SDC) that involved the simulation\n\nand analysis of the Space Shuttle Orbiter Digital Data Processing\n\nSystem (DDPS)\nThis dynamic loading analysis was performed for\n\nSegments\n\nthe NASA Johnson Space Center under contract NAS9-15010\nof the Ascent Test (OFT) configuration were modeled utilizing the\n\nInformation Management System Interpretive Model (IMSIM) in a\n\ncomputerized simulation modeling of the OFT hardware and software\n\nworkload.\n\nSystem requirements for simulation of the OFT configuration were\n\ndefined, and sensitivity analyses determined areas of potential\n\ndata flow problems in DDPS operation\nBased on the defined system\n\nrequirements and these sensitivity analyses, a test design was\n\ndeveloped for adapting, parameterizing, and executing IMSI, using\n\nvarying load and stress conditions for model execution\nAnalyses\n\nof the computer simulation runs are documented herein, including\n\nresults, conclusions, and recommendations for DDPS improvements\n\n\n18 February 1977\n\nSystem Development Corporation\nTM-(L)-5813/O00/O0\n\nii\n\nTABLE OF CONTENTS\n\nSection\n\nPage\n\nSECTION Corporation\n\nTM-(L)-5813/O00/O0\n\n\nSUMMARY\n\n\nThis report summarizes the results of a simulation analysis of the Space\n\nShuttle Orbiter Digital Data Processing System\nThis study was performed for\n\nthe Systems Analysis Branch of the Avionics Systems Engineering Division of\n\nNASA\'s Johnson Space Center. The study was conducted by members of the Systems\n\nAnalysis Center, Systems and Space Programs, of System Development Corporation\n\nunder contract NAS9-15010, and was performed during the period of 19 July 1976\n\nthrough 18 February 1977.\n\nNASA Technical Monitor has been Mr Carroll T. Dawson of the Systems Analysis\n\nBranch\nUnder Mr. Dawson\'s direction, SDC has performed an extensive simulation\n\nmodeling analysis utilizing the IMSIM simulator\nTotal emphasis has been on\n\nthe ascent phase aspects of the orbiter\'s Digital Data Processing System (DDPS)\n\nSDC personnel involved in this study and primary responsibilities were\n\nRichard W Bilek - Head, Systems Analysis Center\noverall project\n\nsupervision and quality assurance\n\nJacobus J Lagas - Project Manager: requirements definition, test\n\ndesign, execution, and analysis.\n\nJames J Peterka - model adaptation, execution, and analysis\n\nDennis A Becker - requirements definition, sensitivity analysis, and\n\nmodel parameterization.\n\n1 1 OBJECTIVES\n\nAs the end product of this contract, this report constitutes an analysis of\n\nthe ascent portion (OPS 1) of the Orbital Flight Test configuration of the\n\nSpace Shuttle\'s DDPS, and identifies constituents of the system which are\n\npotentially subject to overload under stress and which may significantly degrade\n\nperformance of the system in a critical situation\nThe analysis is based on a\n\nquantitative representation of the DDPS as a discrete simulation model and on\n\nthe results derived from the operation of this model. This report also includes\n\na qualitative study of the system organization and structure to determine the\n\nadaptability of the system to varying loads and requirements\nThis information\n\nwas used to parameterize the model and was instrumental in completing the\n\nanalyses\n\n1 2 DATA SOURCES\n\nSources for the study included current documentation of the DDPS OFT functional\n\nsubsystem software requirements documents (FSSRs) and detailed design specifi\xc2\xad\ncations as listed in appendix F Upon NASA direction, the study was primarily\n\nconfined to the hardware and software which may be employed during the ascent\n\nphase of the Orbital Flight Test (OFT). These efforts were applied to investi\xc2\xad\ngation of characteristics and activities which are discernible to a time\n\nresolution of 1 millisecod; i.e., items such as control signals, 1OP memory\n\naccess for commands, parity checking, and CPU instruction execution were\n\nconsidered only insofar as collective effects are concerned. The effort was\n\n\n18 February 1977\n\n1-2\n\nSystem Development Corporation\n\nTM-(L)-5813/O00/O0\n\n\nfocused on quantitative data processing aspects of DDPS; i.e., data flow,\n\nthroughput, response, etc., rather than upon planned information content or\n\nquality, reliability, human engineering, or other more qualitative aspects.\n\nLevel A Hardware soecifications for Anoroach and Landinq Test (ALT) and the\n\nFSSR System Interface documents for \'LT were used in nart to determine the\n\nDDPS OFT hardware configuration and the nature of the comnonents to be\n\nThese\n\nconnected to the GPCs via data buses for communication and control\ndocuments, emoloyed earlier by SDC to develop the simulated hardware config\xc2\xad\nuration depicted in reference 4, were used in conjunction with additional\n\ndetailed information contained in supplementary hardware soecifications\n\n(see references 12, 15, and 21) to determine the simulated hardware confiq\xc2\xad\nuration for OFT\nThese sources also provided information on the orocessing\n\nrates of the Central Processing Units (CPUs), capacities for data retention by\n\nterminal elements such as displays, transmission rates for components and data\n\nbuses, and sizing of message transmissions. Documents pertaining to the CPUs\n\nand Input-Output Processor (lOP) functional descriptions and principles of opera\xc2\xad\ntion were consulted to gain an understanding of the functioning of these modules.\n\nLevel C Software FSSRs for OFT Guidance, Navigation, and Control (GN&C)\n\nplus computer program development specifications for OFT were used to determine\n\nthe structure of the DDPS software\nSignificant program modules (viz. the\n\nPrincipal Functions that execute during the Ascent Phase) to be executed in\n\nthese simulations were also determined from these documents\nFor each of these\n\nprogram modules, characteristics were dezermined with regard to the conditions\n\nfor executing the module, the impact of system status on the execution time of\n\nthe module, the effect that execution of the module has on the system status,\n\nand the data transmissions performed by the module\nThis information was used\n\nto compile a set of system states and to estimate program execution times for\n\nthe DDPS which were principal factors in determining system loading\n\n1 3 MODEL DEVELOPMENT\n\nThe information which was derived from study of the source documents as described\n\nin section 1 2 was used to adapt and parameterize a discrete event simulation\n\nmodel of the DDPS\nThe basic model is a computer program simulator for infor\xc2\xad\nmation management simulation, denoted as "I1SIM" (Information Management System\n\nInterpretive Model)\nThis program was originally developed by SDC under contract\n\nto NASA to provide methods and capabilities for performing dynamic loading\n\nstudies of computer-based data processing systems, and has been well suited to\n\nthe simulation of the Shuttle Orbiter DDPS\nIMSIM is described in detail in\n\nthe IMSIM Users Manual (reference 2), and is summarized in section 5.1.3.\n\n\n18 February 1977\n\n1-3\n\nSystem Development Corporation\n\nTM-(L)-5813/O00/O0\n\n\nHardware characteristics of each component of the DDPS were transcribed to\n\nIMSIM input specification forms, and configuration specification forms were\n\nused to specify the connection of terminal elements and memory units to data\n\nbuses The DDPS components so represented were the Display Electronic Units,\n\nDisplay Units, Multiplexer/Demultiplexers, Display Driver Units, Keyboard Units,\n\nPCMMUs, Engine Interface Units, Master Event Controllers, and the Memory Units\n\nThe data buses themselves were represented as IMSIM\n\nand CPUs of the GPCs\nin the model (see appendix B for a detailed listing of IMSIM input\n\n"datalinks"\nspecifications)\n\nDepiction of the software for simulation is somewhat more complex than the\n\nhardware representation. It is necessary to exercise value judgement in\n\ndeciding whether a program module is to be individually represented, combined\n\nwith other modules for collective representation, or excluded from the model\n\nModules such as the Ascent Digital Autopilot require significant time for\n\nexecution, but involve no change in system state which would affect loading.\n\nOn the other hand, modules such as Redundant Set Launch Processing Sequence\n\ncause a significant change of state when executed but involve only inconse\xc2\xad\nquential execution time. Some modules are called by several other modules,\n\nsuch as Selection Filtering, while others are called by only one.\n\nSoftware is described for IMSIM in terms of schedulable "tasks", loadable\n\n"routines", mathematical expressions or tables which yield execution time as\n\na function of the model state, and logic sequences which manipulate the system\n\nstate. It was necessary to map the salient software characteristics of the\n\nDDPS into IMSIM counterparts so as to retain a meaningful correspondence\n\nbetween system and model constituents, while conforming to the rules and con\xc2\xad\n(It should be noted that this situation is common\n\nstraints imposed by IMSIM\nto all modeling processes, regardless of the tools used, since a model is\n\nnormally intended to be only a suitable approximation of an actual system\n\nSchedulable processes such as SPECS, OPS, and cyclic executives were designated\n\nas IMSIM tasks, and logic sequences were developed to activate and schedule\n\nthem as a function of the simulated clock, externally introduced events, or\n\nthe simulated system state (Major Mode or generated events). Program modules\n\nwhich are executed for a specific task, or for a specific set of tasks, were\n\nFor each routine, a mathematical\n\ncollectively described as "routines"\nalgorithm was prepared which indicates the amount of computation to be simu\xc2\xad\nlated when a task which employs the routine is activated, as a function of the\n\nMore than one routine may be employed\n\nsystem state at the time of activation\nFor each DDPS program module which significantly\n\nin performance of a task\nalters the system state when executing, a similar change was programmed into\n\nIMSIM as a logic sequence, and was synchronized for concurrent execution with\n\nthe appropriate task\n\nSizing of program modules was not a significant factor for the model, since\n\ndynamic memory allocation and loading are not characteristics of the ascent\n\nphase(OPS I) of OFT. They therefore have no impact on system loading. (Flight\n\n\n18 February 1977\n\n1-4\n\nSystem Development Corporation\n\nTM-(L)-5813/O00/0O\n\n\nphase transi-tions involving phases which precede and follow the ascent phase\n\nHowever, such trans\xc2\xad\n(OPS 1) would involve memory management considerations\nitions were outside the scope of this study.)\n\nData transmission within the DDPS is described to IMSIM in terms of "messages".\n\nA message can define a set of transmissions, whether parallel or sequential,\n\nand with varying origins and destinations. All transmissions simulated for\n\nthe DDPS are between the memory of a GPC and some other unit (e.g., a MDM or\n\nPCMMU, or even another GPC memory in the case of intercomputer communication).\n\nSimilar transmissions, such as reading of data from the three IMUs, are described\n\nby a single message which represents concurrent transmissions from FFOI, FF02,\n\nand FF03 (see appendix E for abbreviations) to the GPC memory. Messages are\n\nassociated with tasks and are synchronized to task performance; e.g., if\n\nperformance of a task is deferred or interrupted for higher priority processing,\n\nits associated transmissions may be delayed (but not interrupted).\n\n1.4\n\nAPPLIED WORKLOADS\n\n\nThe workload specification for the DDPS model is actually an integral part of\n\nthe software representation as discussed in section 1.3, but it must be activated\n\nand controlled by an event schedule which effectively specifies parametric\n\nvalues for the simulated software. For each Major Mode, an "Event mask" was\n\ndeveloped that contained every event that would normally occur in the ascent\n\nsequence in that Major Mode. For Major Mode 101 (Terminal Countdown) the event\n\nmask was set through the event schedule that formed part of the job-schedule.\n\nFor Major Modes 102--First Stage, 103--Second Stage, 104--OMS 1 Insertion,\n\nand 105--OMS 2 Insertion, the event masks were set by the event generation logic\n\nthat was incorporated in IMSIM for this study. The event scheduling was designed\n\nto provide a realistic sequence of events, although the time scale was comoressed\n\nfor the sake of efficiency. The compression reduces the simulated time between\n\nevents but provides sufficient intervals to permit loading peaks caused by the\n\nevents to run tnemselves out. Details concerning job schedules and scenarios\n\nappear in section 5.2 4.\n\n1 5 DYNAMIC SIMULATION\n\nSDC performed a series of computer runs with the DDPS model for validation and\n\nverification against predicted performance under normal loading. The automatic\n\nmonitoring and data reduction facilities of IMSIM were augmented with special\n\nsoftware probes and reports to obtain the maximum of useful information from\n\nthe runs, and to simplify extrapolation of results to predicted performance of\n\nthe DDPS. IMSIM is a discrete event simulator and generally functions in a\n\ndeterministic mode, although random behavior can be simulated by drawing\n\npseudorandom numbers from built-in random number generators. Randomness was\n\nincorporated in the delivery of calculated execution times for some routines\n\nand is discussed in sections 5.1.4.2 and 5.2.1.4.\n\nOne major simplification of DDPS simulation was introduced for the computer\n\nruns. Since four of the General Purpose Computers (GPCs) of the DDPS are\n\nall organized as a redundant set for the OFT, they must necessarily perform\n\n\n18 February 1977\n\nSystem Development Corporation\n\n1-5\nTM-(L)-5813/O00/O0\n\n(page 1-6 blank)\n\n\nidentical functions in close synchronization. In fact, the GPCs are precisely\n\nsynchronized in the model unless a perturbation is explicitly introduced. Thus,\n\nno additional information is obtained from simulating the functions of four GPCs\n\nin a redundant configuration as opposed to a single GPC insofar as processor\n\nloading is concerned. Since simulation of parallel computations must be performed\n\nserially on a simplex computer, it is both cost-effective and efficient to elimi\xc2\xad\nnate the redundancy in the model. Note, however, that intercomputer communication\n\nis still simulated among the four GPCs in order to achieve a realistic load on\n\nthe ICC data buses and to properly represent ICC activity for the GPCs.\n\nAnother major simplification was introduced for most of the computer runs.\n\nDuring the first of the series of simulation runs, SDC determined that the\n\ninput-output activity associated with the principal functions has negligible\n\nloading impact on the CPUs\nSince most of the input-output logic is handled\n\nby the lOPs, the only expected effects were some interference between an lOP\n\nand a CPU in accessing memory, and idling of a task while it awaits I/0 comple\xc2\xad\ntion\nSome runs were performed both with and without simulated data trans\xc2\xad\nmission to verify that CPU loading due to transmission is insignificant, and\n\nsimulation of transmission was omitted in subsequent runs. This change greatly\n\nspeeded up simulation and allowed effort to be concentrated on execution of\n\nprincipal functions by the CPU.\n\nData produced from each simulation run include a history of the important\n\nevents and activities, a summary of the final state of the model, and statistics\n\non resource utilization and software functions\nSnapshot dumps were often\n\ntaken of the dynamic state of the model in order to investigate particular\n\nsituations in more detail\n\n1.6\n\nSIMULATION ANALYSIS\n\n\nResults of simulation runs were analyzed in accordance with the approach de\xc2\xad\npicted in section 5 1 4 5 to determine how the simulated DDPS performed under\n\nspecified workloads and what workload variations should be considered for\n\nThe data from history outputs (see appendix C) provide\n\nsubsequent runs\nspecific information on task contention for resources and the maximum inter\xc2\xad\nThe history output also provides\n\nference in performing each type of task\nvaluable insight regarding patterns of behavior in DDPS operation and situations\n\nof peak strain. The summary results (see appendix D) provide information on\n\nbacklogging of tasks for CPU service, delays incurred in performing I/0, system\n\nSystem\n\ncomponent utilization, and statistics on contention for resources\nstatus information yielded clues as to potential system behavior under differ\xc2\xad\nent conditions, which could then be imposed for subsequent simulation runs.\n\nIn consequence, data were accumulated from the series of runs which describe\n\nthe DDPS model behavior and performance under a variety of stress situations.\n\nSubject to the conditions and assumptions detailed in section 5, SDC is\n\nconfident that the model accurately reflects the operation of the DDPS, and\n\nthat the results described in section 2 are indicative of the expected oper\xc2\xad\national performance of the DDPS for the OFT ascent phase\n\n\n18 February 1977\n2\n\n2-1\n\nSystem Development Corporation\n\nTM-(L)-5813/O00/O0\n\n\nRESULTS\n\n\nA series of simulation runs was made with the DDPS model to determine perfor\xc2\xad\nmance during the Ascent phase of the OFT. Since it was not practicable to\n\nsimulate the entire OPS 1 (even with limited variations), portions of the\n\nsequence were identified as being of special interest and a variety of runs\n\nperformed for them\nThe periods of OPS 1 selected for studyt are shown in\n\nfigure 2 1 They include the part of the Terminal Court (MMIOl) between\n\nEvents 6 and 15, the transition to First Stage Flight (MMI02) from SSME\n\nstart (Event 17) through activation of the SRB separation monitor (event\n\n25), transition to Second Stage Flight (MMl03) beginning with function moding\n\nfor separation (Event 27) and proceeding through enablement of MPS dump (Event\n\n33A), and transition to OMS Insertion (MM104) until OMS ignition command\n\n(Event 37)\n\n2.1\n\nDATA TRANSMISSION\n\n\nSeveral runs were made to determine DDPS loading due to data transmission, in\xc2\xad\ncluding intercomputer communication The heaviest loading was obsered for the\n\nsimulated period of the Terminal Count (MM1OI) for reasons which are discussed\n\nin section 2 3 The results for this period are presented in table 2.1\n\nNotably,\nPCMMU #1 and its bus showed\n\n24% utilization,\n\nICC buses showed\n\n17% utilization, and\n\nDisplay Electronic Units showed\n\n17% utilization\n\nPCMMU and ICC figures compared reasonably well with results obtained from the\n\nDDPS Loading Analysis for the ALT (see reference 5) which showed 18% and 15%\n\nrespectively\nThe new figures result from refinement of the workload specifi\xc2\xad\ncations for the model, and are more accurate\n\nThe 7% utilization of memory for transmission shown in table 2.1 is not as\n\nsignificant as it might appear\nThe access rate for memory is considerably\n\ngreater than the addressing rate of which the CPU is capable, and, therefore,\n\nthe rate of processing is not noticeably affected by memory "cycle stealing"\n\nfor transmission\n\nSeveral comparative runs were made with and without simulation of data trans\xc2\xad\nmission to determine the impact of transmission on execution of principal\n\nfunctions\nThe only effects to appear were in delays of up to 2 ms in\n\nstarting principal functions at their appointed times, and task waiting periods\n\nof up to 5 ms for end of transmission\nSince the former can be considered as\n\nscheduling offsets for cyclic processes, and the latter do not affect pro\xc2\xad\ncessing, the majority of simulation runs were conducted without simulating\n\ntransmission in order to concentrate attention on processor utilization and\n\nperformance of principal functions\n\ntThe selection process is described in section 5.2.4.\n\n\n18 February schedule. Prior to Event 14 (T-8 sec),\n\nCPU utilization is less than 90%, but the peak loads which occur every 1000 ms\n\ninterfere with Cyclic Display Processing, causing it to miss a cycle\nThe\n\naverage delay incurred by principal functions in obtaining CPU service is 20 ms,\n\nincluding delays for scheduling and following interruption for execution\n\nof higher priority functions. These delays affect the lower priority functions\n\nwhich generally have the larger cyclic periods, thus, the delays are not\n\nespecially significant\nFurthermore, suitable offset scheduling can reduce\n\nthese delays considerably\n\nCyclic Display Processing was assigned a very low priority even though it is\n\non a 100 ins scheduling cycle\nIt is probable that a suitable upward shift\n\nin its priority would allow it to complete operation in every cycle, without\n\ncausing abortion of other functions\n\nSubsequent to Event 14, Ascent Navigation is activated every 4 seconds\nThis\n\nprincipal function (15) is a heavy user of the CPU and it occupies all avail\xc2\xad\nable CPU time for a varying period every 4 seconds. The period is roughly\n\nestimated to be 1 second, depending on randomness incorporated in the Ascent\n\nNavigation computation time and the CPU requirements of higher priority\n\nfunctions\nIt should be noted that activation of Ascent Navigation occurs\n\nat the confluence of all cycles except the 320 ms cycle; however, the latter\n\noccurs 160 ms afterward, and also contributes to overloading in this situation.\n\nThe effect of the overload is to inhibit performing of any principal functions\n\nbelow priority level 38 for roughly 1 second out of every 4 The panel labeled\n\n"MMIOI" in table 2-2 provides a summary of activity for the principal functions\n\nduring a compressed-time simulation of the countdown from T-19 seconds to T-5\n\nseconds\nAlthough the overload condition is apparent from the entries at\n\npriority levels 36 through 6t , the time compression distorts the results\nIn\n\nfact, CPU percentage for function 14 (Ascent Navigation) extrapolates to approx\xc2\xad\niinately 15%, with a correspondinq decrease in CPU idle and CPU utilization for\n\nlower priority functions\nWhile more function activations would be recorded in\n\nan expanded-time simulation, the number of missed cycles would also increase for\n\nfunctions below priority level 38. Above this priority level, the number of\n\nactivations would also increase, but there would be essentially no other changes.\n\nSimulation of the Terminal Count phase shows an 89% loading of the duty cycle\n\nof the CPU\nThe above -mentioned problems highlight the fact that this is an\n\noverall average, and that for periods of 1000 ms or more, the CPU is 100% loaded\n\nObviously, the CPU usage would be greater than 89% if the additional execution\n\ntime for aborted functions were included. This loading exceeds the system per\xc2\xad\nformance requirement for CPU usage, stated as 70% in reference 10.\n\ntln\n\nIMSIM, a higher numerical value for a priority level indicates a model\n\nduring first and second stage flight (MM102 and MM103). The CPU becomes sat\xc2\xad\nurated upon transition from Terminal Count (MM101) to First Stage Flight (MM102)\n\nand remains in this condition for the periods of MM102 and MM103 that were\n\nstudied (see figures 2-2 and 2-3) As a consequence, certain principal func\xc2\xad\ntions do not complete execution within their duty cycles. In the DDPS model,\n\nsuch a development manifests itself through the abortion of any principal func\xc2\xad\ntion which is scheduled when it has not yet completed execution for a previous\n\ncycle. Functions are never cancelled, only deferred, so, normal operation of\n\nthe model shows the number of times functions were aborted, but not the degree\n\nto which CPU requirements are exceeded.\n\nTable 2-2 shows a performance summary of functions during portions of MMl02 and\n\nMM103 for a nominal ascent profile\nNote that functions begin aborting\n\n("Missed" column) at absolute priority level 124 in MM102 and at 118 in MM103.\n\nThese correspond to the Guidance/Control Steering Interface and SRB Data\n\nAcquisition, respectively\nThe proportion of abortions progressively in\xc2\xad\ncreases through lower priority functions until level 50. Functions with\n\nabsolute priorities below 50 never complete a cycle in MMI02\n\nTable 2-2 also shows the percentage of total CPU\neach function. Since the CPU becomes saturated,\nused to compare the relative CPU requirements of\nabortions\nIt is evident from inspection of the\n176--the Ascent Digital Autopilot--is by far the\nwith Principal Function 15--Ascent Navigation--a\nMM103\n\n\ntime used in the execution of\n\nthese figures should only be\n\nfunctions which suffered no\n\ntable that Principal Function\n\ngreatest employer of the CPU,\n\nsignificant second during\n\n\nBecause of CPU saturation, it is pointless to attempt to analyze DDPS loading\n\nunder other conditions (i e , for other than a nominal operational profile).\n\nSome simulation runs were made which include SSME failure (Vehicle Safing)\n\nand faulty thruster indication, but the results are not significant under the\n\ncircumstances\nFurthermore, statistics on data transmission and data bus\n\nutilization are of little value, since transmission initiations are distri\xc2\xad\nbuted among most principal functions\nTransmissions related to the high\xc2\xad\npriority functions are adequately covered by simulation of the Terminal Count\n\nphase, and other transmissions are not accurately represented in the flight\n\nphases due to the CPU overload condition\n\nin order to get some estimate of the CPU overload, a series of simuation runs\n\nwere undertaken with a modified DDPS. These runs give a better idea of where\n\nthe CPU time is being spent. Note that the Ascent Digital Autopilot still\n\noccupies 45% of the CPU time in MMl02, and Ascent Navigation remains essentially\n\nconstant at 14% in MMI03\n\nFrom the information obtained through high execution rate simulation, it can be\n\ndetermined that the CPU is overloaded by a factor of 1.8 during some major cycles\n\nof MM102 and by 2.3 in MMI03. If the NASA requirement of a 30% reserve capacity\n\nis included for the CPU, the overload factor is 2.6 for MM102 and 3.3 for MMI03.\n\nWhile this type of simulation run was not performed for MM104 (Orbit Insertion),\n\nit is apparent from the data in table 2-2 that a similar overload develops, with\n\naborts beginning at priority level 116, corresponding to the Ascent RCS Command\n\nSOP.\n\nTransport lag and initiation jitter for the flight control executive were not\n\nspecifically investigated in this study, since they were analyzed in the earlier\n\nALT dynamic loading analysis (see reference 5), and the analysis is valid with\n\nrespect to OFT.\n\n\nSystem Development Corporation\n\n\n3\n3.1\n\nTM-(L)-5813/000/00\n\n\n3-1\n\n18 February 1977\nCONCLUSIONS\n\nSYSTEM CAPACITY\n\n\nThe data bus network is only lightly loaded and is configured so that virtually\n\nno message congestion occurs. The ICC buses operate at capacity during memory\xc2\xad\nto-memory transmission and impose the most significant I/0 loads on memory during\n\na 2 ms period of every 40 ms cycle; however, this is estimated at 60% of memory\n\naccesses for 5% of the time, or 3% of the capacity for memory access. Data buses\n\nare estimated to have reserve capacity for transmission in excess of 75%.\n\nIn summary, the capacities to perform data transmission in the GPCs and the data\n\nbus network appear adequate to support the countdown and ascent phases of the\n\nOrbital Flight Test.\n\nLoading for the CPU appears to significantly exceed the 70% limit requirement\n\nimposed by NASA during the terminal countdown, and to greatly exceed the capacity\n\nof the CPU during first and second stage flight, as summarized in table 3-1.\n\nTable 3-1.\n\nCPU Loading\n\n\n% CPU REQUIRED\n\n\nMM103\n\n\nMM102\nMM1O1\nGeneral\n\nGeneral\n\nWorst\nMajor Cycle\n\nGeneral\n\nWorst\n\nMajor Cycle\n\n\n100% CPU\n\nAvailable\n\n89\n\n128\n\n180\n\n108\n\n230\n\n\n70% CPU\n\nAvailable\n\n127\n\n183\n\n257\n\n154\n\n329\n\n\nIn fact, even the total capability of the CPU is inadequate for the ascent\n\nfunctions. Of course, these observations are based on the detailed assumptions\n\nand estimates regarding principal function operating characteristics included\n\nin sections 5 1.4.2 and 5.2.1.4; in particular, the values are sensitive to\n\nestimated computation time for the Ascent Digital Autopilot, Ascent Navigation,\n\nand Cyclic Display Processing.\n\nGiven the above information, we conclude that NASA requirements for OFT software\n\nas presently detailed in references 6 through 12 cannot be met by the DDSP under\n\nthe operating constraints imposed in reference 18.\n\n\n18 February 1977\n\n3.2\n\n3-2\n\nSystem Development Corporation\n\nTM-(L)-5318/O00/O0\n\n\nRESPONSE CHARACTERISTICS\n\n\nSince the System Software Interface Processor is assigned the highest oriority\n\nof scheduled processes, it is executed on schedule and can respond to 1/0 com\xc2\xad\npletion with virtually no delay Offset adjustments in the schedules for the\n\nsecond and third highest oriority cyclic processes--the Fast Cycle Executive\n\nand the Minor Cycle Executive--should make it oossible for both of these oro\xc2\xad\ncesses to execute on schedule, and for the Fast Cycle Executive to respond\n\nimmediately to I/0 completions\n\nResponse of other processes generally deteriorates as priority decreases. Be\xc2\xad\ncause of the CPU overload, responses can only be meaningfully discussed in re\xc2\xad\ngard to the highest priority processes. While these experienced delays of up\n\nto 20 ms, offset scheduling can probably reduce the delays to less than 10 ms.\n\n\n18 February 1977\n\n4-1\n\nSystem Development Corporation\n\nTM-(L)-5813/O00/O0\n\n\n4. RECOMMENDATIONS\n\nThe recommendations presented in this section reflect the results of analyses\n\nof the simulation runs performed under this study and indicate suggestions\n\nrelating directly to the planned OFT hardware and software, as well as recom\xc2\xad\nmendations for effective expansion of this study effort. Section 4.1 deals\n\nwith specific configuration recommendations, while section 4.2 presents a\n\nsummary of augmented study tasks to refine these analyses and to investigate\n\nrelated aspects in other flight phases\n\n4 1 DDPS CONFIGURATION RECOMMENDATIONS\n\nThe CPU overload situation discussed in section 3 calls for a careful review\n\nof assumptions and timing estimates used in the DDPS simulation, and prudence\n\ndictates at least preliminary effort toward reducing the CPU load requirements\n\nIn both of these activities, emphasis should\n\nduring the flight phases of OFT\nfirst directed to the principal functions which appear to place the most\n\nbe\nsevere loading on the CPU during critical periods, viz , the Ascent Digital\n\nAutopilot (DAP, Ascent Navigation, and the Cyclic Display Processor.\n\nSDC recommends that the following methods of attack be considered:\n\n* \tReassess the estimated computation times and derivations as given in\n\nsection 5 2.1.4\n\nReassign priorities to give heavy CPU users the lowest possible\n\n* \t\npriorities, consistent with criticality and frequency of execution.\n\nRestrict the execution of subfunctions of the principal functions to\n\nmultiples of the fundamental cycle period of execution of the function,\n\nas is done through use of Hybrid Dispatcher in the Approach and Landing\n\nTest (ALT) software\n\n" Reconsider required execution rates for some of the more time-consuming\n\nprincipal functions\n\n" Reexamine the total OFT software package, with a view toward eliminating\n\nnonessential processing\n\n* Shift nonessential processing and functions which do not require full\n\nredundancy for reliability to the backup GPC.\n\n" Reduce DDPS redundant operation to obtain more CPU power\n\n\n18 February 1977\n\n4-2\n\nSystem Development Corporation\n\nTM-(L)-5813/O00/O0\n\n\n4 2 AUGMENTATION OF SIMULATION EFFORTS\n\nThe dynamic, discrete simulation model of the DDPS was developed by SDC to\n\nIts operation has been verified and\n\nfulfill the objectives of the DDPS study\nvalidated against requirements and available performance data. SDC recommends\n\nits continued use as a device for experimenting with scheduling algorithms and\n\napplied workloads for the DDPS under a variety of conditions which would be\n\ndifficult or impossible to verify prior to actual flight. The monitoring and\n\nreporting facilities of the model could not be effectively incorporated in the\n\nreal system, no other approach can enable system designers to obtain more in\xc2\xad\nsight into the dynamic behavior of the DDPS during its development. Use of\n\nthe model also provides project management with an overview of the dynamic,\n\nas well as static, character of the DDPS\n\n4 2 1 Extended Studies for OFT Ascent Operations\n\na\n\nContinued use of the model should be performed on the Ascent phase (OPS 1)\n\nconfiguration to include solutions to the apparent problems of software\n\nexecution uncovered during this study, as well as to incorporate up-to\xc2\xad\ndate information on detailed design specifications and refined estimates\n\nof program module timing\nAny of the alternatives or combination of\n\nalternatives given in section 4 1--DDPS Configuration Recommendations-\xc2\xad\nthat are considered as desirable optiQns to alleviate the problem of\n\nsoftware execution under normal conditions should be modeled and simu\xc2\xad\nlated to verify that they actually resolve the execution problem\n\nTo accomplish an extended analysis of Guidance, Navigation, and Control\n\nfunctions, some changes to the existing parameterized model will have\n\nto be made\nMost of the hardware specification parameters (IMSIM forms\n\n6 through 14) will remain unchanged\nIf necessary, speedfactors and\n\naccess times can be changed with ease\nSome of the software workload\n\nparameters, however (IMSIM forms 2 through 5)swill require more exten\xc2\xad\nsive modification\nReassessment of the estimated computation times\n\ngiven in section 5 2 1 4 may require modification of parameters on input\n\nform 3 and in the Variable Expressions file\nReallocation of priorities\n\nto Ascent DAP and Ascent Navigation will require changes to the parameters\n\non input form 2, changes to the Variable Expressions file, and new logic\n\nto be incorporated in IMSIM\nReconsideration of lower function execution\n\nrates will require changes to the activation logic described in section\n\n5 2 1 3 Elimination or combination of certain functions, resulting\n\nfrom reexamination of the total software package will-require changes\n\nto parameters for input forms 1, 2, 3,and 5\n\nEfforts to be accomplished should include aspects of the following\n\nactivities, as were performed under this study\n\n1 \n Requirements analysis\n\n2 \n Test design\n\n\n18 February 1977\n\n4-3\n\nSystem Development Corporation\n\nTM-(L)-5813/O00/O0\n\n\n3 \t Model adaptation and parameterization\n\n4 \n Model execution\n\n5 \n Test analysis and documentation\n\nb \t Further effort should be expended to determine the greatest stress situ\xc2\xad\nations which can develop during the OFT mission, and results should be\n\nemployed in constructing associated workloads for the model\nAdditional\n\nconditions which should be investigated via simulation include system\n\nerrors and component failures. Once the normal execution of all Principal\n\nFunctions within their prescribed cycles is accomplished, the uncommon\n\nsituations, such as Vehicle Safing, OMS failure, etc , should be simu\xc2\xad\nlated according to precise schedules to achieve maximum impact\n\n4 2 2 Investigations of Entry, RTLS, and Orbit Operations\n\na \t Detailed simulations and analyses should be conducted into OPS 3 (Entry\n\nOperations) and OPS 6 (Return to Launch Site Operations), at a sufficient\n\nlevel of detail to assess the performance characteristics of these\n\ncritical phases during high load conditions\nThese simulations would be\n\na natural evolution from OFT Ascent Operations loading analyses, since\n\nall OFT hardware components of the model, a subset of the OFT aDolications\n\nsoftware, and the DDPS execution logic have already been developed and\n\nvalidated. In addition, several unique features have been incorporated\n\ninto the IMSIM logic to more accurately model the specific characteristics\n\nof the DDPS. These capabilities will all be instrumental in effectively\n\nassessing the throughput and loading performance of the DDPS during Entry\n\nand RTLS. Moreover, since the development of applications software for\n\nthese operations is nearing completion, reasonable estimates of program\n\nexecution times can be employed to provide additional confidence in simu\xc2\xad\nlation run results\n\nAs has been done for OFT Ascent Operations and the ALT configuration,\n\nsuch simulations should be employed to evaluate the effects on the DDPS\n\nthroughput performance due to the interaction of the GPCs, the data\n\nbuses, the various BTUs (bus terminal units) when driven by the software\n\nneeded to meet the functional requirements identified for these oDerations.\n\nThe end product of this activity should be a detailed analysis of the\n\norbiter DDPS performance during applicable modes, identifying areas\n\nor functions with high probabilities of incurring degradation due to\n\noverloading of the DDPS during periods of high dynamic activity.\n\nb \t A further logical extension of the OFT Ascent Onerations studies would\n\ninvolve the throughout analysis of OPS 2 (Orbit Operations), and\n\npossibly OPS 4 (Atmosoheric Operations), if this latter ohase is actually\n\nincorporated into orbiter capabilities as a distinct operational phase\n\n\n18 February 1977\n\n4-4\n\nSystem Development Corporation\n\nTM-(L)-5813/O00/O0\n\n\nAs was stated above for Entry and RTLS Operations, such simulation\n\nanalyses should be based on the currently available IMSIM simulation\nmodel which has been extensively adapted and parameterized for OFT\n\nanalysis. As additional applications software is produced to accommodate\n\nthese operations, expected program execution times can again be incorp\xc2\xad\norated as model input parameters to provide more accurate representations\n\nof software component characteristics.\n\nc\n\nSpecific IMSIM loading studies should be performed to assess the perform\xc2\xad\nance of the DDPS when transitioning from one OPS to another\nIn contrast\n\nto the studies outlined in a and b above where memory management is\n\nnot a consideration (i e , all pertinent software modules are assumed to\n\nbe core resident during the operations), main memory loading and consol\xc2\xad\nidation may pose new problems during OPS transitions\nParticular re\xc2\xad\nconfigurations that should be studied are as follows\n\n1 \t From OPS 1 (Ascent Operations) to OPS 2 (Orbit Operations).\n\n2 \t From OPS 2 (Orbit Operations) to OPS 3 Entry Operations) with\n\nreturn to OPS 2 (event 61)\n\n3. \t\nFrom OPS 3 (Entry Operations) to OPS 4 (Atmospheric Operations)t\n\n.\n\n\n4 \t From OPS 1 ( scent Operations) to OPS 6 (Return to Launch Site\n\nOperations,)\n\n5 \t From OPS 6 (Return to Launch Site Operations) to OPS 4 (Atmospheric\nOperations) t\nSuch simulations should emphasize software loads occurring before and\n\nafter reconfigurations, with particular attention being directed towards\n\nprocessor utilization, data bus utilization, and task completion delays\n\nthat may result from these transitions\n\nd \t Operational Flight Test requirements for the DDPS should be analyzed\n\nfrom a dynamic functional standpoint to determine behavior with an\n\nadditional simplex (nonredundant) mode GPC and software execution for\n\nnew major functions and modes\nThe impact of additional dynamic loading\n\nfor activities such as uplink-downlink, fault detection, and payload\n\nmonitoring should also be investigated\n\ne \t While the DDPS model is well suited to the investigation of dynamic\n\nfunctions at a resolution of 1 ms, it should not be used at other\n\nappropriate time scales to represent specific functions of the DDPS.\n\nFunctions which are independent of each other, or at least series\xc2\xad\nrelated, should be individually modeled as required to observe their\n\nindividual behavior\n\n\ntApplicable\n\nonly if OPS 4 is eventually incorporated as a distinct operational\n\nphase for OFT\n\n\n18 February 1977\n\n4-5\n(Page 4-6 blank)\n\n\nSystem Development Corporation\n\nTM-(L)-5813/O00/O0\n\n\nSDC has designed the DDPS model to represent the functions of the GPCs,\n\nthe bus network, and bus terminals as an integrated system in which feed\xc2\xad\nback is an essential characteristic. Localized activity, such as occurs\n\nin DEUs, IOPS, and PCMMUs, may normally have negligible impact on the\n\nDDPS operational at the 1 ms level of discrimination, but may still\n\nrequire simulation to determine situations in which they become saturated\n\nor otherwise loaded so as to change their operating characteristics and\n\naffect general system performance\nThe precise steps by which the Process\n\nManagement component of the Flight Control Operation System monitors\n\nevents and schedules processes should be simulated in detail to determine\n\nperformance and dynamic loading conditions, and used as an aid in makinq\n\nsystematic, effective improvements in scheduling algorithms and methods\n\nof implementation. In this manner, a variety of aspects of synchronous\n\nand asynchronous approaches can be evaluated effectively.\n\nf. SDC also recommends that consideration be given to the construction of\n\nnew specialized models of system components to study their behavior\n\nthrough simulation on appropriate time scales (e g , to a microsecond\n\nlevel)\nSuch models may be built using IMSIM, as was the DDPS model, or\n\nthey may be constructed using the underlying general-purpose simulation\n\npackage--MODLIT--upon which IMSIM is based\nBoth of these tools may be\n\nused to construct models which can be operated dynamically by discrete\n\nsimulation to yield useful data on behavior under conditions which are\n\ndifficult or impossible to duplicate in real systems\nFurthermore,\n\nprocesses such as intercomputer communication may be represented at more\n\nthan one level of time resolution in different models\nFor example, one\n\nmodel can be used to determine "macroscale" characteristics for inclusion\n\nin another model which uses a finer resolution factor.\n\n\n18 February 1977\n\n5-1\n\nSystem Development Corporation\n\nTM-(L)-5813/O00/O0\n\n\n5. TECHNICAL DESCRIPTION\n\nThe following paragraphs describe in detail the objectives of the Timing Sensi\xc2\xad\ntivity Analysis Study and the efforts performed under each of the tasks defined\n\nin section 3 of the Statement of Work\n\n5 1 INTRODUCTION\n\nHigh-speed digital computers have been increasingly applied to the analysis and\n\ndesign of complex systems\nOne of the most useful techniques for such applica\xc2\xad\ntions is that of discrete simulation, in which the system is represented in the\n\ncomputer as a dynamic model which changes its state with the stepwise passage\n\nof simulated time\n\nThe IMSTM model has been developed to aid in the investigation of systems which\n\ninclude computers\nIt is constructed upon the MODLIT Discrete System Simulator\n\nIn effect, IMSIM is a general model of a computerized transmission system, which\n\ncan be tailored to represent a wide variety of configurations, components, and\n\napplied loadings. Furthermore, as a fully interactive model, it enables the user\n\nto monitor its behavior and to make dynamic modifications during simulation\n\nThe objectives of the Timing Sensitivity Analysis Study and the model goals are\n\npresented in section 5 1 1 The guidelines and assumptions for the model\n\ndevelopment are delineated in section 5.1 2. A brief conceptual overview of\n\nIMSIM is given in this introduction in section 5 1 3, and the overall approach\n\nto the model development, applied workloads, and dynamic simulation is given in\n\nsection 5 1.4.\n\n5 1 1 Objectives and Model Goals\n\nThe primary objective of the Space Shuttle Orbiter Digital Data Processing\n\nSystem Timing Sensitivity Analysis effort is to investigate the dynamic behavior\n\nof the orbiter\'s data processinq subsystem durinq the Ascent phase (OPS 1) of\n\nthe already defined OFT in order to identify and formulate resolutions for\n\ncritical Derformance areas\n\nTo meet this objective, the generalized IMSIM model was adapted and parameterized,\n\nso that the Space Shuttle\'s appropriate hardware and the software Principal\n\nFunctions for OFT were properly represented in this model\n\nThe model goals were established as a result of the work performed under the\n\nRequirements Definition task (Statement of Work task 3 1).\n\n\n18 February 1977\n\n5-2\n\nSystem Development Corporation\n\nTM-(L)-5813/O00/O0\n\n\nFrom a study of the overall dynamic hardware and software data flow require\xc2\xad\nments it was determined that the IMSIM model should be constructed within the\n\nfollowing set of basic goals\n\n" \t\nThe model should be configured so as to allow statistical data\n\ngeneration on the dynamic behavior of central processing units, which\n\nwill be the focal point for analyzing system performance.\n\n* \t\nSuspected potential data flow problem areas (defined by a Sensitivity\n\nAnalysis) should be modeled such that data could be generated to determine\n\nif and/or to what extent these areas are critical in respect to system\n\nperformance\n\n* \t\nThe model should be desiqned for the specific operational confiquration\n\n(OPS I - Ascent phase) and include only that hardware and software required\n\nto simulate the functional dynamics required for that operation, i.e.,\n\nShuttle Orbiter Data Processing Subsystem characteristics such as oper\xc2\xad\national reconfiguration, fall and fault redundancy, abort modes, and BITE\n\nshould not be incorporated in the model.\n\n5 1 2 Guidelines and Assumptions\n\n5 1 2 1 Model Guidelines\nThe following NASA-specified guidelines for the IMSIM\n\nmodel were defined in a project coordination meeting held August 11-13, 1976, and\n\nin subsequent coordination communications\xc2\xad\na \t The simulation model will be parameterized and adapted for the Ascent\n\nphase of the OFT configuration\nMajor emphasis will be on Major Modes\n\n101, 102, 103, and 104\n\nb \t Events in Major Mode 101 will start at T-20 with event No 6--as specified in\n\nSS-P-0002-51OD, Computer Proqram Development Specification, OFT Level B Guidar\n\nNaviqation, and Control already used dated 30 Auqust 1976, page 3-41\n\nc\n\nPrelaunch events and activities prior to transition to Major Mode 101 will\n\nbe urovided by NASA for setting initial conditions.\n\n\nd \t In the OFT Ascent phase, four GPCs will be in redundant mode, while GPC\n\n#5 will act as backup and will not be simulated\n\ne \t The Manipulator Control Interface Unit (MCIU) is connected to the Launch\n\nData Buses (LOB 1 and LDB 2), but need not be simulated.\n\nf. \t\nEvents can be scheduled in a condensed timeline\nwere sent to NASA for perusal and approval\n\n\nThese timelined events\n\n\n18 February 1977\n\n5-3\n\nSystem Development Corporation\n\nTM-(L)-5813/O00/O0\n\n\ng \t After the Solid Rocket Booster (SRB) Separation, event sequences can be set\n\nup with nominal time.\n\nh. acquire an understanding of the nature of the data processing system\n\ncharacteristics required for its adaptation and execution\n\nIMSIM is a data system analyzer for modeling a wide range of computer configur\xc2\xad\nations, software workloads, and executive program algorithms\nIt is a tool\n\ndesigned to provide the analysis needed to determine and/or verify the oper\xc2\xad\national capability of the hardware, workload, and executive control elements of\n\na data processing system to meet functional requirements\nThe IMSIM simulator\n\n\n18 February 1977\n\n5-8\n\nSystem Development Corporation\n\nTM-(L)-5813/O00/O0\n\n\nis a discrete event loading analysis model based on data traffic that flows\n\nthrough static elements\nIt is a large scale computer program compiled in System\n\nDevelopment Corporation\'s MODLIT language which supports production runs and\n\nreal-time interactive use.\n\nThe principal elements of the IMSIM Simulator are described in the subsections\n\nthat follow.\n\n5 1 3.1 Hardware Representation\nThe equipment simulation categories used in\n\nIMSIM cover five basic types of equipment\nmemory units, storage units,\n\ncomputer processors, data transmission links, and a group called "devices" that\n\nincludes all hardware not covered by the other four categories\nAlthough there\n\nis no theoretical constraint upon the organization of processors and memory\n\nunits, attempts by the computing industry to design control programs (operating\n\nsystems) for various configurations of computers have resulted in the definition\n\nof a substructure for large computer systems\nTo realistically represent such\n\nsystems for simulation, IMSIM includes the concept of the "virtual machine":\n\na computer in which at least one processor can access all memory units. In its\n\nsimulation runs, SDC has simulated the Space Shuttle\'s digital data processing\n\nsystem as one Virtual Machine with four GPCs\n\nStorage units and devices are generally considered as global (systemwide)\n\nsystem components, but can be viewed as local (to a virtual machine) when\n\nconnected exclusively to a machine via data transmission links\nProcessors\n\nand memory units are always considered as local components. Data links are\n\ndefined for use in connecting any comnonents except processors, and have either\n\nlocal or global status, depending upon the configuration.\n\nInputs to IMSIM include the means for soecifying characteristics for individual\n\nmembers of each of the component types mentioned, together with a description\n\nof the way in which they are to be configured\nOne other type of component is\n\ndefined in IMSIM and is classified as hardware\nthe "data set"\nA data set may\n\nbe viewed as a subdivision of a storage unit, and is intended to correspond to\n\na file of data to be stored in the unit\n\n5 1 3 2 Software and Workload Representation. In order to study the dynamic\n\nbehavior of a system representation by IMSIM, it is necessary to anly a work\xc2\xad\nload to the system\nA workload structure has been incorporated in IMSIM which\n\nresembles that of the actual comnuter system\nIt includes general building\n\nblocks for rudimentary representation of comnuter programs and data and for des\xc2\xad\ncribing data transmission, and the means of organizing these elements into a\n\nhierarchical structure which is consistent with the hardware representation. The\n\nbuilding blocks are denoted as routines, data blocks, and messages. These are com\xc2\xad\nbined into "tasks" which ar3 units of work to be performed by a single virtual\n\nmachine\n\n\n18 February 1977\n\n5-9\n\nSystem Development Corporation\n\nTM-(L)-5813/O00/O0\n\n\nTasks, in turn, are organized as a time-distributed network of steps which are\n\ncollectively denoted as a "job", a job is a unit of work to be performed by the\n\noverall system. This organization is depicted in figure 5-1.\n\nRoutines and data blocks are always considered to be local elements of virtual\n\nmachines, while messages may have either global or local significance, depending\n\nupon their individual characteristics and the hardware configuration\nInputs\n\nto IMSIM provide the means for specifying characteristics of individual sharabil\xc2\xad\nity among concurrent tasks\n\n\nSystem\n\n\nJob\n\nRouine\n\n--\n\noesate\n\nne\n\n\ndenotes descent in the workload hierarchy\n\n\nFigure 5-1.\n\nWorkload Structure in a Simulated System\n\n\n18 February 1977\n\n5-10\n\nSystem Development Corporation\n\nTM-(L)-5813/O00/O0\n\n\n5 1.3 3 Functional Description. The functional logic which is incorporated\n\nin IMSIM includes representation of hardware behavior, applications programs,\n\nand executive software\nDistinguished system comoonents--whether hardware\n\nor software--are represented by suitable MODLIT entities such as facilities\n\nand storages\nBoth the logic and the system components are generalized IMSIM\n\ncapabilities which must be tailored to suit the system to be simulated. For\n\nthis reason, IMSIM is designed to operate in two phases- initialization and\n\nsimulation\nIn the initialization phase, IMSIM receives and processes "forms"\n\nwhich complete a system definition and describe a workload to be applied to\n\nthe defined system during simulation\nCertain system specifications can also\n\nbe processed during simulation, thereby permitting the dynamic modification of\n\nthe system.\n\nThe logic of IMSIM is expressed in terms of MODLIT logic blocks, and can be\n\nsubdivided into eight sections\n\na. Processing of input specifications\n\nb\n\nProcessing of job requests\n\n\nc. Task preparation\n\nd\n\nTask execution\n\n\ne. Element space allocation\n\nf\n\nMessage preparation\n\n\ng. Message transmission\n\nh\n\nTask removal\n\n\nThe first of these eight sections constitutes the initialization ohase\nA\n\nportion of this phase, together with the other seven sections, comprise the\n\nsiiimulation phase\n5 1 3 4 Preparation of Model Specifications\nThis section describes the various\n\nspecification forms which were used to complete the definition of IMSIM for\n\nrepresentation of the Space Shuttle\'s DDPS and to define software and workload\n\ncharacteristics\n\nEach parametric input form is represented by one or more lines of input. All\n\ninput lines are interpreted on a free-field basis, 1 e , one or more spaces\n\nseparate successive fields\nOnly the first 71 characters of each line are\n\ninterpreted\nPositions 1 through 70 contain the information to be read, position\n\n71, if occupied by any character other than a zero, indicates that the next line\n\n\n18 February 1977 \n\n\n5-11\n\nSystem Development Corporation\n\nTM-(L)-5813/O00/O0\n\n\nin the input sequence is a continuation of the current line\n80 are used for sequence numbers.\n\n\nPositions 72 through\n\n\n" ) in position\n\nA double prime (\' \') in positions 1 and 2 or a quotation mark\n1 indicates that the line is a comment which is used solely to annotate printed\n\noutputs.\n\nThe first field of each form contains an integer which identifies the form type.\n\nThe layout line which follows the column headings line of each form description\n\nindicates the magnitude of values for each field (e.g , nnnnnn) and signifies\n\nnnn. means a fractional value is\n\nthe optional use of a fractional value\nThe column\n\npossible, while nnn means that only integer values should anpear\nheadings X44, X45, X56, and X57 represent Saves cellst which correspond to storage\n\nlocations within MODLIT. The parameters X44, X45, X56, and X57 will be assigned\n\nthe values appearing under these headings for use in a MODLIT variable. The\n\nMODLIT variable, specified under the column heading V is then computed as a\n\nfunction of the values in the Savex cells.\n\nThe 14 input forms that follow depict the formats necessary for input to IMSIM\n\nForms 1 through 5 are used to described the simulated workload characteristics,\n\nwhile the remaining 9 forms are used to describe the hardware configuration.\n\n\nt Savex\n\ncells are specially designated MODLIT data base entries used for\n\na variety of computation and control purooses\n\n\n18 February 1977 \t\n\nFORM 1 - JOB DEFINITION\n\n\na\n\nForm Job\n1\n\nSystem Development Corporation\n\nTM-(L)-5813/O00/O0\n\n\n5-12\n\nnn\n\nE.G.\n\n1\n1\n1\n1\n\n2\n2\n2\n3\n\nImmediate Predecessors\n\n\nTask Type\n\nPriority Nature Go/NoGo\n\nnn \t\n\nn\n\nnnn\n\nnn nn .. Corporation\n\nTM-(L)-5813/O00/O0\n\n\n5.1.3.5 Preparation of a Job Schedule and Event Occurrences. The Job Schedule\n\nprovides the means to initiate jobs, add job and task definitions, modify or add\n\nsystem specifications, and specify events through setting of Savex cells. Job\n\nstart times are specified which trigger the commencement of selected jobs. As\n\neach job is initiated, tasks and message transactions are generated and executed,\n\nresources are scanned for availability\nSpecific events which are to occur within\n\njobs are defined as event times for the setting and modification of Savex cells.\n\nThe Job Schedule is read during the simulation phase, and job schedule forms\n\nshould be ordered on the time field. No job or event should be scheduled to\n\nstart before simulated time 20, since a line which begins with a number less\n\nthan 20 is treated as a specification form. The executive (job 1) is automati\xc2\xad\ncally started and needs no job initiation in the schedule. In subsequent runs,\n\nthe input schedule may be varied to study the effects of different job execution\n\nsequences while keeping the basic hardware and prototype software configurations\n\nfixed.\n\nThe two forms that follow depict the formats necessary for job initiation and\n\nevent occurrence\n\n\n18 February 1977\n\na\n\n5-26\n\nSystem Development Corporation\n\nTM-(L)-5813/O00/O0\n\n\nJOB INITIATION\n\nTime\n\nnnnnn\nEG\n\n150\n160\n210\n\nJob \t Trigger\nMessage\n\nnn\n\n2\n\n3\n6\n\nRepeat\n\nFlag\n\n\nnn\n\nn\n\n\n28\n12\n\n0\n\n1\n\n\nTime \t\n\nThe time (in milliseconds) at which the job is to introduced to the system\n\n\nJob \t\n\nThe number of the job (see Form 1) to be initiated\n\n\nTrigger Message \t\n\nIf this field is specified, it must contain the number of a message (see\n\nForm 5) which must complete transmission after the given time, in order to\n\nstart the job\nIf unspecified, the job will be started immediately\n\n\nRepeat Flag\n\nApplicable only if a trigger message is specified\no - the job is initiated once, following the next completion of the\ntransmission of the specified message\nI - the job is to be initiated following every occurrence of the\nspecified message transmission\n\n5-27 \t\n\n18 February 1977\n\nb\n\nSystem Development Corporation\n\nTM-(L)-5813/000/00\n\nEVENT OCCURENCE\n\n\nTime\n\n0\n\nEvents \t\n\nEvent K\n\n\nSavex\n\nIncrement \t\n\nSavex\n\nIncrement\n\n\nnnnn\n\nnnn\n\n\n681\n681\n\n10\n\n1\n\n\nnnnnn\n\n0\n\nnnnn\n\nnnn \t\n\nEG\n\n1800\n1950\n1960\n\n0\n0\n0\n\n688\n680\n688\n\n+2\n\n1\n-1\n\nTime \t\n\nThe time (in milliseconds) at which the indicated events occur\nthan 19\n\n\nSavex \t\n\nThe number of the Savex cell associated with the event\n\n\nIncrement \t\n\nThe amount by which the Savex cell is to be changed\n\n\nIt must be greater\n\n\n18 February 1977\n\n5-28\n\nSystem Development Corporation\n\nTM-(L)-5813/000/00\n\n\n5.1 \t Overall Approach\n\n4\nIn coordination with NASA a simulation model version was developed for the\n\nSpace Shuttle, reflecting the hardware characteristics and the functions to be\n\nperformed during the Ascent phase (OPS 1) of the Orbital Flight Test for the\n\nSpace Shuttle Orbiter.\n\nThe \t arameters for the hardware, that were contained in the ALT simulation\n\np\ntest (see reference 4) and were the same for the Orbital Flight Test, were\n\nused in the OFT simulation. The parameters for the additional hardware that\n\nhas \t\nbeen used in OFT were extracted as follows:\n\na. \t\nFor the Master Events Controller (MEC) from references 15 and 21\n\nb \t For the Engine Interface Unit (EIU) from references 12 and 15.\n\nThese parameters are discussed in detail in section 5.2.3.\n\nCharacteristics relating to the expected performance of software programs,\n\nmodules, and cyclic executives have been based largely on the Principal Functions,\n\nas described in detail in the FSSR documents for Level C, Guidance, Navigation,\n\nand Control in the OFT for the Space Shuttle, and the OFT Function Level\n\nRequirements for GN&C in the Computer Program Development Specification Volume\n\nV, Book I, SS-P-002-510D. Approximation of total number and type of instruc\xc2\xad\ntions for each routine for each Principal Function were determined\nRefined\n\ntiming of the execution phase of each routine was derived by determining the\n\nexecution time for each set of instructions that operate under certain speci\xc2\xad\nfied conditions, based on the instruction execution times given in references\n\n26 through 28 and reference 30, and execution times given in reference 27--both\n\nin appendix D (table D-2), parts 1 and 3 of volume 2.\n\n5 1 4 1 Approach to Hardware Definition. The simulated configuration is based\n\non the OFT configuration of the Space Shuttle Orbiter\nIt consists of the\n\nfollowing with the number in parentheses indicating the entity ident number on\n\nthe IMSIM input specification forms\n\nCertain entities are simulated separately as their characteristics and functions\n\nare distinct and logically different even though they are physically constructed\n\nas a unit, e g., the GPCs are logically depicted as consisting of a CPU, a Core\n\nMemory, and an IOP.\n\na. \t\nFour Space Shuttle Advanced System/4P1 Model AP-1OI Central Processing\n\nUnits (90001, 90002, 90003, 90004). software requirements for the\n\nSpace Shuttle Orbiter onboard digital data processing system specify a hier\xc2\xad\narchical system to be developed according to established techniques of structured\n\nprogramming\nThe activity within an individual computer (GPC) of the DDPS\n\nessentially consists of a set of tasks which may be performed concurrently\n\n(i.e., multitasking) and which compete with each ocher for use of the central\n\nprocessor. The tasks are assigned unique priorities to be used in resolving\n\nconflicts over the CPU, and they are scheduled either by time pulses or by the\n\noccurrence of specific events\nAll input/output control is handled by lOPs,\n\nthereby relieving the CPU of these specialized functions\n\nDefinition of an IMSIM workload to represent this activity necessitated\n\na\n\nestablishment of specific objectives for simulation,\n\nb\n\nan understanding of the organization and intercommunication of the software,\n\nc\n\ninformation and assumptions concerning the amount of computing and data\ntransmission performed as a function of the state of the system,\n\nd\n\nvalue judgments as to relative significance of functions, events, states,\netc , to the simulation objectives, and\n\ne\n\nmethods to be used for representation of each of the significant aspects\nof the system\n\nThese five areas of concern are interdependent and had to be treated in parallel,\n\nfor example, it is impractical to gather detailed information and make assumptions\n\nabout a software module which is essentially irrelevant to simulation goals.\n\nSimulation objectives were tentatively established and are presented in sections\n\n5 1.1 and 5 1 2 The methods for software representation are described in\n\nsection 5.2 3.3\nSince virtually all of the activity within the DDPS is organ\xc2\xad\nized into schedulabld "processes", these processes are identified, together with\n\nthe\'conditions for activating them, and the program modules which are executed\n\nfor them The processes are associated with three areas. User Interface, System\n\nControl, and Guidance Navigation and Control\n\nFor convenience, a table containing all the Principal Functions in the ascent\n\nphase with the corresponding task numbers and
